--- 帖子 #952 ---
My post #8 was this safety checklist. It is still totally applicable, even to managing the EA. I pasted it here for convenience.

============================
Safety checklist

In my strategy there are no strict rules – you have to understand what is happening on the chart and use the trading tool between your ears. But there are some basics, which I call safety checks. These are things every trader should use for every trade. Basic common sense. I think that if you applied these safety checks properly, you could probably trade ANY system successfully.

1. Dominant trend: Check the trend direction on the next time frame up. I use H4, so I check what is happening on D1. It doesn’t have to be officially up/down yet just as long as it is clearly heading in that direction. It also helps to have a look at what is happening on the next TF down (in my case it’s H1). Sometimes you can see details there that are not clear on your trading TF.

2. S/R obstructions: Avoid trading into support or resistance – this could be horizontal levels or inclined levels as in trend lines or channels. Also, what looks like a strong uptrend on H4 might just be a pullback leg on D1 within a down channel. It would be foolish to buy at the upper boundary line joining successively lower swing high points.

3. Enter when price is close to the EMAs, which means you should avoid entering when there is open space between price and the EMAs. Here the rubber band is stretched, so price is likely to bounce back before it continues with the trend.

4. Avoid long/explosive bars, enter where the bars are short or “quiet” (and such bars are usually close to the EMAs).

5. Avoid trading into price extremes – look at the monthly or weekly chart to see if price is at a long-term low/high.

6. Beware of "old" trends. No trend continues forever, so if a trend has continued for a long time (e.g. it starts in the lower left corner of your screen and goes all the way up to the top right corner) it is an old trend, probably getting tired, its life expectancy is reducing rapidly (as with all of us eventually ). It might continue still, but as a safety precaution, just walk away. Wait for a fresh trend to start.

--- 帖子 #954 ---
Yeah, it's not necessary to drop timeframes. You might end up getting prematurely stopped out and inducing analysis paralysis. If you're going to drop down, I'd advocate just dropping down 1 TF (D1 to H4 or H4 to H1). The whole point of 8-20ema system is to hop in on a trend. If you start to get finicky about getting a tighter entry or stoploss, you're either going to miss the move or get stopped out, possibly multiple times, eroding the simple edge in this strategy.

--- 帖子 #961 ---
Hi AlistairC and all, thanks to Alistair for starting this thread. This takes me back to my beginnings in trading and it's refreshing to revisit some simple yet powerful strategies.

I've read through most of the pages, and one website Alistair shared was an article on The Floor Trader Trading System (sorry I can't find it now), but that article describes 3 Levels of trade entries, which I thought was really helpful. I'll attach a chart to help illustrate. Basically the concept is if price retraces deeply back to/into/slightly past the 20 EMA, that provides a potential Level 1 Entry. If price retraces to just touching the 9 EMA, that provides a potential Level 2 Entry. If price pulls back but does not touch the 9 EMA (meaning it's a small pullback/retracement), that provides a potential Level 3 Entry. Each have their merit/strengths/weaknesses in different situations, but I thought it helpful to me at least to think of the amount of pullbacks in 3 different categories.

Chart attached.

MantisClaw

--- 帖子 #966 ---
I have posted a video below that you must watch, and wait for the second half where he tells you what he does.

What you need to know about our TS is that (if I have understood things correctly) it does not move until price is in profit AND the latest fractal has formed also in the profit zone. The TS sleeps until those conditions are met. The 3x3 EA uses a clever idea for a TS because it moves the stop down the swing points (by using fractals). In other words it is dynamic, or responsive to market behaviour. I have tried to improve it by asking eurusd-thijs to set the TS with an option to use the previous fractal, not the current one, which is often too close, but then I drop down one TF (H1, not H4, in my case) to make it more responsive, or to use a finer set of moves.

This is the theory! But read further ...

Trailing stops have this aura of making you much more money than a fixed stop, but it's often not like that. They really only help you when you get a nice long run, but how often does that happen? If you trade short-term as I do then TSs are a waste of time. If you want to trade longer-term (as I would like to do!) then it's worth experimenting with a TS. The problem is, if you set it too tight, you get stopped out too early, if you set it too wide, you leave half your profit on the table.

What you really need is a TS that tightens itself as price moves. It should start very wide (e.g 100 pips) then tighten up after every xx candles, to eg 80 pips, then 60 etc. But getting the timing right is not going to be easy. The PSAR TS does this and I have tried using it, mostly unsuccessfully, even though visually it makes so much sense. I will attach a copy of the PSAR TS below.

My solution, for want of anything better, is to move a trade to B/E at 50 pips and then set a fixed stop at 100 pips. That seems to be a good number most of the time. When the markets are jumpy I will make that less, like 80 pips or whatever the chart structure dictates. For longer term trading I prefer to move my stop manually using my own assessment of the market. And it's fun to do that!

Inserted Video

--- 帖子 #1000 ---
Just some thoughts

After 10 winners in a row last week, this week has given me so far 2 losers and 1 winner. What are my conclusions?

1. I was thinking that maybe if you leave EA work at random you get opposite trades and it might be better to only sell or only buy certain pairs. However: as the results show, I have a losing trade buying the USD (agains CAD) as well as a winning trade buying the USD (against JPY), so I will keep the buy AND sell open on all pairs. We are trading short term, so let this clever system decide instead of giving priority to my perception of what is likely

2. I don't think you can prevent a losing trade but I would like to reduce the amount lost in a losing trade. For this purpose before implementing EA on a currency pair I will now change the following : a) since EA will trade on a pullback, the fact that the price continues in the wrong direction proves the trade wrong, starting from a position that is already a certain number of pips away from the previous candle. Therefore I will no longer use the same number of pips for SL and TP but instead I will program higher TP (2 to 1 or at least 1,5 to 1) to compensate the previous candle and b) I will also calculate in advance the maximum loss / profit that goes with the number of pips chosen for SL and TP and perhaps use this data to chose a higher or lower number of pips for SL / TP. Just trading 0,01 lot of EUR/GBP with a 75 pip SL resulted in 8,72 euro loss, which I consider too high. c) As a general rule I also tend to choose the number of pips in function of the ADR, we like the trade to close the same day so if the ADR is high my SL / TP is higher and vice versa

Hope this is useful and of course your more professional and experienced thoughts on this are more than welcome.

Apologies for my absence since last week. I have been travelling: Cape Town, Johannesburg, Nairobi, Zanzibar. I have been looking at examples of where the EMAs first become aligned. Almost always there is a pullback after about 10 to 20 bars and that seems to be an excellent place to enter. Sometimes the pulback is just an inside bar or two-bar pullback, others it is a full pullback to the EMAs. It's always wise to give away a few pips with the entry position so that a small bounce doesn't trigger the trade too early. Where price pulls back into or below the EMAs I wait for price to close on the trend side of the 8 and 21 EMAs.

On this EURJPY chart I used large yellow arrows to mark when the EMAs first become aligned (it is basically when the 21 EMA crosses the 100 EMA). I deliberately chose a messy bit of price action, price goes sideways most of the time on this chart. Yet there were no losses. By being patient and waiting for the pullback to present an entry opportunity I was protected from being stopped out. It's not always like this. Go and do your own back-testing and see for yourself.

--- 帖子 #1014 ---
This is the original brief. I want to focus on point 2: "Place a trade at the close of a candle that opens inside the fast EMA and then closes outside of it (i.e. in the direction of the trend)."

This is based on my observation that a pullback to the EMAs will often take price into the EMA 'envelope' and the best time to enter is when price breaks out of the envelope and closes outside (on the trend side) of the two EMAs. This means that in most cases the entry is close to the EMAs and price is not stretched away / overbought/sold. This is more than just a convenient trigger, it is strategic, it increases the odds of success (as I have observed it). It also means that in most cases the three EMAs have started separating and have fallen into a clean trend.

I have marked some of these below. If my logic is correct then, ideally, the trigger candle should be on the trading TF (H4 in my case). But then there will be very few signals, so we need to drop down a bit. The question then is how far down the TFs should we go so that we get enough signals but still make use of the breakout candle concept. I don't know, but it seems to me that the sweet spot is probably M30.

But having said all of that, unless you have millions to spend, any EA that we are able to develop can never be as good as manual trading. The issue with this EA (and any EA) is that it can't be left on its own and needs to be managed.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

Note that in this case the 'pullback' became a fairly lengthy period of sideways movement. Therefore I expected a decent breakout. But, to avoid triggering my trade too early, I placed my entry some distance away from the consolidation area. In this case my entry was about 10 to 12 pips above the tips of the main group of candles.

--- 帖子 #1023 ---
Those blue arrows are built into whatever App you are using to place your trade. In this case it must be the EA. I haven't seen them while using the EA either, but they are actually quite common. They are useful to mark your trade beginning and ending with a dotted line between them - you can use them to analyse the trade after it has closed. Some traders leave them in place over a period of time as data to review. I don't like the extra clutter so I delete them as soon as I can (using Ctrl-B as a shortcut).

--- 帖子 #1031 ---
I have been exploring my "yellow arrow strategy" (I must find a better name for it!) that I first commented on last week. It is simply just the moment when all three EMAs align on that TF. Once the signal comes it is important to wait for a pullback and then find a suitable entry (which includes checking the HTF to avoid trading against the trend).

It had seemed to me that the trigger candle that I requested in the EA, where the candle opens inside the fast EMA and closes outside (on the trend side), should be an important feature, but whenever I look for an example, I am not sure about that.

Look at this case. There was the yellow arrow, then a bit later a pullback. I marked what I consider the ideal entry candle with a small yellow box. The next large green candle qualifies as a true "trigger candle" by the EA definition, and for the purposes of coding an EA, it's fine to use it. But for manual trading I think we can do better. The marked candle hasn't closed outside the 8 EMA, so I would have placed the buy stop order a few pips, maybe 5 pips) outside.

--- 帖子 #1046 ---
Good work mate. I think there's nothing wrong sticking to basics and MA crossovers are one of the most fundamental indicator based signals out there. I have been just recently doing some reading on trading indexes for the long term based on golden crosses and death crosses. And they are not so far-fetched, in fact, quite profitable.

So why not apply it on some of other financial securities? One caveat would be the misleading volatility and not so reliable signals if you try to apply it down to very short time frames like minutes and whatnot. But most of the time, it could work out in your favor with the daily and hourly timeframes.

Arguably, some of the allegedly premium and winning indicators that are sold on some charting websites are also mainly based on moving average cross-overs in the background.

We use EMAs to help us define trend. Another standard method is to use higher highs and higher lows to define an uptrend, and vice versa. The only time I would switch over to candles would be to see the close of the breakout candle in order to place a trade there. Do you think you could get your brain around using just line graphs?

--- 帖子 #1087 ---
I decided to go back to the original method I started this thread with. In fact post #1 summarises it nicely. I have borrowed text from that post to explain what I am doing now.

===========

Borrowed from post #1: This chart summarises my strategy. I wait for a new trend to form, as defined by the EMA crossover, then wait for a pullback to (or close to) the EMAs, and then enter a trade. I almost always use stop orders so that if a pullback turns into a reversal, there is less chance of that trade being taken out as price continues the 'wrong' way. Note that I identified a two-bar pullback setup and two inside bar setups. They all represent a pullback, or at least a pause, or a local consolidation period. And after the multi-IB setup, price exploded away. That's just perfect. I look for that kind of setup all the time (and some of my recent trades make this obvious - waiting for a consolidation and trading from just below/above it is a powerful strategy).

Something I didn't comment on in post #1 is the importance of trading in line with the higher timeframe (HTF). However, it is not necessary to wait for the HTF to be "officially" in the same direction as the trade (e.g. EMAs crossed, or a HH/HL setup or similar), just that it is obviously heading in that direction. But to make it more certain and less based on instinct, experience or the trader's discretion, one can insist on seeing an "official" matching trend direction.

Attached Image (click to enlarge)

We have breakout!

This is what usually happens after a period of consolidation, whether that consolidation is a short, local one or days- or weeks-long one. And, while we don't want to get caught in a period when price goes nowhere, so too must we welcome such times because we can benefit from the breakout. I got caught up in one H4 pullback bar, but luckily, it didn't last long.

--- 帖子 #1122 ---
Actually, the level where you entered might be better than anything slightly higher up, for the simple reason that if the price action continues going sideways for any length of time after triggering your trade, then it is stressful for the trader waiting and hoping, and what if for some reason price does pull back more deeply just there (Murphy's Law at play), then you are really sweating. Better to be slightly further away and be triggered by the actual breakout candle. That's the perfect scenario.

--- 帖子 #1137 ---
Thanks for your questions, L,,Olonese. Let me say that I am not a detail kind of person, and like to keep things as simple as possible. Call me lazy if you like, but trading is difficult enough at the best of times, so why add complexity? That's my view, but it might not work for you. Do what works for you (but keep it simple ).

1. Have you tested if your IB method works in lower TF with 1h IB bars? No, I haven't tested this, but I am certain it will work because it makes little difference what TF you are working on, you get the same patterns again and again. Just that the lower TFs supposedly are less reliable. I'm not sure about that - faster, yes, but if I give you any chart of almost any currency pair but I don't tell you which pair and which TF, you will likely not be able to tell me if it is a higher TF or a lower TF.

2. Since eur/usd gbp/usd nzd/usd aud/usd are correlated, have you tried to verify for example if a movement eur/usd TF 4H in which there is a large movement without IB actually occurred in gbp/usd nzd/usd aud /usd? Again the answer is no, and I don't bother with knowing much about correlated pairs, as they change over time, sometimes very correlated, sometimes less, and keeping track of all that is, in my opinion, too much effort! Perhaps I'm wrong, I don't know and haven't bothered to find out. My mantra is to keep it as simple as possible. If I see a currency pair that meets the conditions, trade it, if not, don't trade it. The only restriction I use is not to trade more than two pairs that are similar, eg AUDCAD and AUDCHF etc.

--- 帖子 #1159 ---
I have copied the safety checklist here. It is from post #8. It's more as a reminder to myself, but I hope it helps you to be reminded

====================================
Safety checklist

In my strategy there are no strict rules – you have to understand what is happening on the chart and use the trading tool between your ears. But there are some basics, which I call safety checks. These are things every trader should use for every trade. Basic common sense. I think that if you applied these safety checks properly, you could probably trade ANY system successfully.

1. Dominant trend: Check the trend direction on the next time frame up. I use H4, so I check what is happening on D1. It doesn’t have to be officially up/down yet just as long as it is clearly heading in that direction. It also helps to have a look at what is happening on the next TF down (in my case it’s H1). Sometimes you can see details there that are not clear on your trading TF. Comment: Based on my conversation with josi above, perhaps also check what the weekly is doing (the HHTF from H4). Also, you might choose to be stricter about the HTF (and also HHTF?) not having to be "officially" up/down.

2. S/R obstructions: Avoid trading into support or resistance – this could be horizontal levels or inclined levels as in trend lines or channels. Also, what looks like a strong uptrend on H4 might just be a pullback leg on D1 within a down channel. It would be foolish to buy at the upper boundary line joining successively lower swing high points.

3. Enter when price is close to the EMAs, which means you should avoid entering when there is open space between price and the EMAs. Here the rubber band is stretched, so price is likely to bounce back before it continues with the trend.

4. Avoid long/explosive bars, enter where the bars are short or “quiet” (and such bars are usually close to the EMAs).

5. Avoid trading into price extremes – look at the monthly or weekly chart to see if price is at a long-term low/high.

6. Beware of "old" trends. No trend continues forever, so if a trend has continued for a long time (e.g. it starts in the lower left corner of your screen and goes all the way up to the top right corner) it is an old trend, probably getting tired, its life expectancy is reducing rapidly (as with all of us eventually ). It might continue still, but as a safety precaution, just walk away. Wait for a fresh trend to start.

--- 帖子 #1161 ---
a man's gotta do, what a man's gotta do!

magical thinking has already caused many a bankruptcy, so off you go...

As to MAs:

let's say price equals MA period 1 (just for fun's sake):

that would mean a D1 simulation on H4 ought to be something like period 6, right (H4x6=D1)

that would mean a W1 simulation on H4 ought to be something like period 30, right (H4x6=D1; D1x5=W1)

that would mean a Mth simulation on H4 ought to be something like period 120, right ((H4x6=D1; D1x5=W1; W1x4=Mth)

So your 8,21,100 are pretty close to the real thing. And what is even better: they don't have to be magical, mystical but simply mathematical... (Fibonacci, of course, is also highly mathematical - just not relevant for trading....)

[P.S.: did you see how USDCHF came back yet again? And now compare this to GBPCHF - this is where the fun was.]

USDJPY H4 - Waiting for retest of D1 trendline and/or break of H4 trendline
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

I scan for trends using 8/20 emas. I draw trendlines and wait for a trendline break. After the break, I will drill down 1 or 2 timeframes below, and enter with an inside bar (Breakout-Pullback-Continuation / BPC) or wait for the retest of the trendline (Breakout-Pullback-Retest - BPR). I use fibs for targets (138.2%). Will elaborate more if I managed to catch any trade.

D1 / H4 / H1 for swing

H1 / M15 / M5 for intraday

Multiple pairs. Choose the nicest looking setups. If you set targets with the bigger timeframe in mind, you should have very favourable R:R from 3:1 to 6:1. Then it becomes an exercise of entering as many good setups as possible to let the edge play out.

Also, take note of correlated pairs and don't enter 2 similar setups with strong positive or negative correlation.

--- 帖子 #1179 ---
Example of target (which i just missed on EURUSD M15 ). I normally target 138.2%. You have to draw the fib in reverse, from the breakout point (100%) to the retracement point (0%). You can see on price just hit 138.2%.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

What's usually happening price breaksout at 100% and rushes towards 138.2%. Then, profit-taking happens and price retraces back to the point of breakout at 100%. At that point, you can consider a second entry (your standard S/R breakout retest entry) but I don't because of unfavourable R:R. You also might get taken out if price retraces deeper.

If you are confident, you can shoot for 161.8% which is also a typical target. A more conservative target is 127.2%. You can consider partials at these different points as well.

Sometimes, I will draw fibs the normal way and shoot for the -38.2%. These offer massive R:R.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

That's it for targets.

--- 帖子 #1193 ---
The Ichimoku system intrigues many traders but I don't know if anyone uses it successfully and consistently. The adaptation shown in the video is basically a single line / price crossover using just one line from the many in Ichimoku, the Kijun-sen.

So it ends up being a variation on what many traders use, which is a single MA system, trading when price crosses an MA. In fact, an MA of 1 is basically the same as the actual price, so we could say this is an MA of 1 period (i.e. price) crossing an MA of xx periods (commonly 50). And so it's not a major digression from what we are doing except that in this video example we are using an Ichimoku line, not an MA . The basic rule for the Kijun-sen system is to enter when price closes on the other side of the Kijun-sen and exit the trade when it does the opposite. There's more to it from about minute 29 in the video where he refers to a baseline.

This is what it looks like - you'll have to click on the chart to see the line because it's quite dark to see on the thumbnail image here.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

--- 帖子 #1201 ---
There's 2 ways of calculating fib projections and they depend on how you draw the fib.

Method 1 - Measuring the Impulse Wave

The standard way would be to draw the fib from the start of the impulse wave at point A at 100% to point B at 0%. Point C would be your retracement. This retracement could coincide with whatever confluence of your liking. S/R, Imbalance, Divergence, Trendline Bounce, Trendline Break. The extra sauce is up to you.

Projected targets (measuring from the 50% retracement)-27.2% - 62 pips -38.2% - 72 pips -61.8% - 94 pips -100% - 125 pips (aka full extension)  Attached Image (click to enlarge)

Method 2 - Measuring the Retracement

This is a more conservative TP approach. Instead of measuring the impulse wave, you measure the correction wave from B at 0% to C at 100% and use fib extensions as targets.

I normally target these 2 fib extensions. Assume we enter at 50% at point C.138.2% - 60 pips 161.8% - 72 pips  Attached Image (click to enlarge)

These are more conservative targets and you're working under the assumption that point C will hold as a swing point. More interestingly, you will see price tend to stall at 138.2% and 161.8%. Of course, that's not always the case, but if you draw these fibs, you'll notice price will very often go to these levels to the pip. On multiple time-frames. There's nothing magical about this. I think they are just very common projection targets.

There's nothing fancy about using fibs. They're really just a simple percentages to measure retracements and projections. The underlying concepts are your basic 123 and ABCD patterns. Nothing ground breaking. 8/20 ema serves as a trend filter. You use fibs to set entries, stoplosses and targets.

A simple positive R:R systemPrice is trending as shown by 8/20 ema cross over. Measure a strong impulse wave.Set limit order at 61.8% SL at 100% TP at -38.2% This will always result in a more than 1:2 RR as long as you trade bigger timeframe   Attached Image (click to enlarge)

You can improve on this with candlestick patterns, confluences, etc. The problem here is that strong trends don't tend to retrace to 61.8%. They tend to be shallower at the 38.2%, sometimes not even. And you would need to target further fib extensions in order to yield a positive R:R.

But the concept is simple. Trends and pullbacks.

--- 帖子 #1203 ---
That's an interesting question and not easy to answer. One answer I found (from a respected source too!) is that a reversal is a change in the price direction of an asset. Well, thanks for nothing!

It depends on your timeframe. A reversal on a 5 minute chart is just a minor blip on a weekly chart, so we need to look at it within the timeframe we are working with and probably on the next higher TF to confirm.

The most useful definition I have seen is that a reversal is when a pullback keeps moving the other way, to a point where the trader believes it is safe to trade it in the new direction. So when do you feel safe to trade a pullback in the 'wrong' direction? That could be:when price closes on the other side a trend line (hardly a reversal unless confirmed with something else really solid) when your preferred MAs have crossed (and should be confirmed with something else depending on whether it is low-number MAs and how many MAs you are using) when you can see a series of higher highs and higher lows after a downtrend or vice versa (and possibly confirmed with something else) When the new trend breaks above the most recent swing point in the old trend (confirmation optional). So has USDCHF reversed? On H4 yes, and yes I would trade it, on D1 it's early days, and my preferred 8/21/100 EMAs have not re-aligned yet, so some might trade this, I am still waiting, on W1, it has crossed the trend line, but has not closed yet so this might become a reversal but it's not 'ripe' yet and we need to wait.

The above is just my ramblings, not an authoritative statement. It would be interesting to hear other people's comments.

--- 帖子 #1207 ---
It's totally none of my business and I don't want to interfere at all. Just a caveat (you know already but maybe not all of your readers)

What strikes me is the fact that you seem to confuse causality and correlation. (As most people tend to do in most areas of life etc)

You came up with an adaptation of a running system and had a good week.

And now you think the dashboard(s) you use have been tweaked in such a way that they can be trusted etc.

These dashboards (and EAs) are - as far as I can see - based on MAs.

That means (logically) that - doing what they do, formula-wise - they produce false entry signals during ranges.

A pretty old study once mentioned that markets trend about 30% of the time and are in ranges about 70% of the time.

If that still holds true that means that - on average - 70% of the time your system will be prone to some false signals.

To me that means two things:

a) you cannot trust the dashboard (EA) because some times it will work pretty well (at other times it may be worse than chance, i. e. without an edge)

b) the adage - always trade in the direction of higher TF - isn't wrong as such but will produce pretty late entries (and bad trades, once the trend has ended but the the end isn't as yet signalled by the MAs)

So the way around this dilemma - I think - is to always look at the charts you are trading. If price is clearly in a trend on H4 (HHs and HLs vs LLs and LHs), then it is likely to continue for the time being - and that's it.

Now you can say i pick a higher average and all is good, price do not touch it. But what have you done? Represents the average now the price action? What will a high average point yet? This is the danger going that way.

Following averages that blind is ignoring the basics. I trade long enough and i have hands down used thousands of combination and hundreds indicators to filter out "false" signals. All these does not the job. The only job that count is to realize that market is build around cause and effect. Cause and effekt. Not build around a mystical combination of averages.

We have a "pinch" on AUDUSD H4 where the EMAs are all close to each other, basically a consolidation. Breakouts from consolidation are very common, so we can expect a breakout at some stage. The trick is to catch it. One option is to place a stop order above/below (or both) of the consolidation, well out of reach of any false breaks. Another is to wait for the usual pullback and then enter. We don't know which way it will break, but as D1, W1 and MN1 are all bearish, my money is on a break downward.

--- 帖子 #1222 ---

--- 帖子 #1223 ---
A pinch is just my term for a normal consolidation, but because we use EMAs in a visual way, we can see that the EMAs are very close together. When we get such a situation, a breakout is very common.

The standard strategy for trading a breakout is to draw trend lines above and below the consolidation and to place a stop order outside one or both of those trend lines. If it is clear which way the break is likely to go, then just one stop order is required. The trick I was referring to isn't a "trick" (sorry, that's a figure of speech one might use in English that doesn't translate well). It is to avoid being prematurely triggered by a false break, so the stop order needs to be a safe distance beyond the trend line.

This H4 example shows a very long pinch and the breakout is explosive. The trend lines help define where the edges of the consolidation zone are and the stop order needs to be (on H4) at least 10 pips away from that. Giving away or sacrificing those 10 or so pips is nothing compared to what we get from the eventual breakout. In the example below the consolidation / pinch goes on for a long time, so it is easy to be triggered far too soon and possibly get into trouble, which is why giving it the extra space is necessary. That's the "trick". The daily chart is below this one and the consolidation zone is just visible in the centre of the chart.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

This is an extreme example of a pinch. Just looking at it we have no idea which way it is likely to break, and looking at the daily it isn't much clearer, except that price does seem to be edging up.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

Then comes the most difficult part: at what point do you decide to stop a trade, USD is very strong, I have a trailing stop at 150 pips, if we get through this point, next target would be 0,94 ... to be or not to be?

--- 帖子 #1240 ---
This is indeed the most difficult part. You're already up by 300 pips - that's an amazing trade already. (Well, I think it's amazing!).

Either you exit according to some known and clear rules, then your own discretion and emotion don't have to come into it. Or else you have to balance out many different factors - is price overbought, is it up against resistance, likely to pullback soon, what does the daily or the weekly tell me about possible future movements, can I cope with the emotional challenge of letting it drop back in the hope or belief that it will continue further up if I leave it long enough? Etc. But I think the main questions now are more about you and your psychological make-up. I know I don't cope well with the stress of leaving a trade running for a long time. So I tend to go for nearby targets and, ideally, get out before the first pullback. But you seem to be made of stronger steel. You have to decide!

--- 帖子 #1245 ---
my thoughts .... always trade in 2 lots ...

(I) in any trend trading system.....

1)  the high of the entry candle ( if you are short )  and the low of the entry candle ( if you are long )  is never broken.... so...

a) entry has to be at the close of the candle. not at the high/low of the candle

b) the initial stoploss should be the high/low of the entry candle with a minimal buffer. ( NOT the swing high/low )

2) TP of one lot (of the 2 lots you have traded) is 1:1(+spread)  ... this is a very tiny target easily acheived.

3) once this is acheived, means that you will not loose in the trade.... your SL is still (( b) the initial stoploss should be the high/low of the entry candle with a                  minimal buffer.) ))

(II) once your trade is at no loss .... now think of profits .........for the remaining one lot you hold

1) target

a) a simple 1:1 R:R / 1:2 R:R

b) use fibo targets

c) use ATR/ADR of the timeframe tou are trading for targets

2)  trailing sl

a) trail SL to 8 ema

b) trail SL to previous candle high/low

c) many a times you will realise that after hitting your trailing sl the trend continues.

i) you are still in profit in this trade so look out for other opportunities

ii) go to lower timeframe and re-enter the trend.

Plan the trade and Trade the plan

--- 帖子 #1257 ---
I use several different templates that I flip through at the change of every 4-hour candle. The 2x3 and 3x3 ones are obvious (I think), but I thought I should show you my 2MA crossover template and how I use it. Those little red/green arrows appear after the 2 MAs have crossed over. We get many of these crossovers on our charts, most of which are not suitable.

The 2MA crossovers that I am looking for are where all three EMAs are aligned, but where the two faster EMAs have just crossed over. In most cases this means that the entry is very early in the new trend.

There are 4 examples on this chart.

1. YES. The first one is great - everything is already below the 100 EMA and the 2MA crossover happens on a bearish pin bar. This crossover also has a small consolidation zone that lasts for 3 more candles after the pinbar and makes a perfect place to enter.

2. NO. Against the dominant trend, EMAs not aligned.

3. NO. The EMAs are not completely aligned.

4. YES. The price bars have dropped through the EMAs, which suggests momentum, and all three EMAs have just come into alignment. You could enter at the base of the crossover candle as it's not too long, or you could enter below the consolidation area that follows in the next 3 or 5 candles.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

--- 帖子 #1263 ---
I have copied the safety checklist here. It is from post #8. I hope it helps you to be reminded

=================================
Safety checklist

In my strategy there are no strict rules – you have to understand what is happening on the chart and use the trading tool between your ears. But there are some basics, which I call safety checks. These are things every trader should use for every trade. Basic common sense. I think that if you applied these safety checks properly, you could probably trade ANY system successfully.

1. Dominant trend: Check the trend direction on the next time frame up. I use H4, so I check what is happening on D1. It doesn’t have to be officially up/down yet just as long as it is clearly heading in that direction, although you might want to be stricter about this. It also helps to have a look at what is happening on the next TF down (in my case it’s H1). Sometimes you can see details there that are not clear on your trading TF.

2. S/R obstructions: Avoid trading into support or resistance – this could be horizontal levels or inclined levels as in trend lines or channels. Also, what looks like a strong uptrend on H4 might just be a pullback leg on D1 within a down channel. It would be foolish to buy at the upper boundary line joining successively lower swing high points.

3. Enter when price is close to the EMAs, which means you should avoid entering when there is open space between price and the EMAs. Here the rubber band is stretched, so price is likely to bounce back before it continues with the trend.

4. Avoid long/explosive bars, enter where the bars are short or “quiet” (and such bars are usually close to the EMAs).

5. Avoid trading into price extremes – look at the monthly or weekly chart to see if price is at a long-term low/high.

6. Beware of "old" trends. No trend continues forever, so if a trend has continued for a long time (e.g. it starts in the lower left corner of your screen and goes all the way up to the top right corner) it is an old trend, probably getting tired, its life expectancy is reducing rapidly (as with all of us eventually ). It might continue still, but as a safety precaution, just walk away. Wait for a fresh trend to start.

--- 帖子 #1274 ---
"Refresh" is just another term for pullback or correction. It can also be called a reset, where a long or "old" trend such as we have on GBPUSD H4 is refreshed and we can then take it as a "fresh" trend. That's the theory according to some that articles I have read. I'm not entirely convinced, but where the higher timeframes are still looking good in a certain direction (bearish in our case), then I guess if H4 pulls back significantly there is no reason why it should not continue in the dominant direction, hence the idea of a refresh or reset.

--- 帖子 #1293 ---
Hi Eod, no I haven't. But here is what I said in my first post of this thread:

I use an 8/21 EMA strategy. There is nothing special about those two numbers. No magic. You could use Phillip's 10/21 EMAs or 10/25 or 6/15, or 12/35, whatever.

More important than which EMAs you use is to stick with the ones you have chosen and get to know their behaviour. You will gain nothing from hopping from one set of EMAs to another trying to get the best ones. There are no best ones.

Adding to this thread: as a 'young' trader, I notice that my main losses have almost always been caused by too tight stoplosses.

When you learn to trade, it is always teached that your TP should preferable be twice your SL.

Of course, it's hard to find obvious trades with decent high TP.

So when your potential TP is eg 100 pips, you should have a SL of no more than 50 pips, this is bound to generate many stopouts.

Would an additional condition to enter a trade not better be to have a SL of at least 1 ADR?

--- 帖子 #1350 ---
I don't have much experience with long-term trades; I try to be in and out the same day or in 2, max 3 days.

However, what I notice on the higher TFs is that they appear to be overbought, as do many of the JPY pairs. Price might well continue up, but my instinct (honed on intra-day trades) is to get out while the going is good.

For the sake of the exercise, go and look at the spaces between the 3 EMAs, even measure the pip distances and compare the current values with what you see in recent chart history. You might find that this pip distance is greater now on W1 and MN1 than on most other "stretched" occasions. And on most such occasions a big pullback is usually the result. On the other hand, it is known that the probability of a trend continuing is greater than the probability of it reversing. On the daily chart the price bars are getting closer and closer to the 100 EMA. Often this is a sign that price is getting ready to drop into and even below the EMAs.

Another useful exercise is to look at all the JPY pairs and then all the EUR pairs. What do they seem to be doing long-term?

But I'm really not sure what your best course of action should be. I can only speak for myself.

--- 帖子 #1368 ---
Yes, it works with indices etc, but it's important to be familiar with the behaviour of these instruments. They behave a bit differently from forex. Also the scale or size of the numbers you work with, and calculating lot sizes, are quite different. So if you are familiar with these differences then there's no reason the method we follow here would not work.

--- 帖子 #1369 ---
I'm busy with an interesting project. Teaching someone to trade who knows nothing about trading and has only a mobile phone to work with.

There are only the basic indicators provided on Android phones, so no EAs or dashboards such as we use here, so I cut it right back to the basics.

Trading timeframe: H4

Higher TF: D1

8/21/100 EMAs on both.

When the EMAs align on both charts (=3x2 method) look for a trigger candle on H4. Trigger candle is one that opens 'inside' the 8 EMA and closes 'outside' it (same as used on our EA).

Yesterday I showed him how and where to place the SL and TP. It's very early days, but exciting to see him grasp this very simple method so quickly.

--- 帖子 #1377 ---
There is always more to learn!

I realised that the trigger candle concept is more useful than I originally thought. This is the concept that eurusd-thijs used for his EA that he very kindly made public on this thread. In the EA I have it set to send a possible trade signal when it opens below the fast MA and closes above it (for a bullish trend). I have marked those that qualify on the chart.

The benefit of using this is that most times it represents a breakout above/below the EMAs, but also ensures that price is not overbought/oversold, so the chance of price pushing quickly away from the EMAs is increased.

I have set eurusd-thijs's EA on just one time-frame (H4 in my case) and the trigger candle on the same time-frame. So when the EA gives me a possible trade signal I am actually being told I have an H4 trigger candle waiting for me to make a decision with. I have also worked out that if the next two higher TFs (D1 and W1 in my case) show just the fast and medium EMAs are also bullish/bearish, then most times I have a good trade waiting.

--- 帖子 #1378 ---
"There is always more to learn"

classical analysis paralysis

1) dont trade the insturment..... trade the timeframe.....

2) develop one strategy/system in a timeframe of your choice....

3) set the settings of your indicators to suit your timeframe.....

4) backtest your indicator settings in the timeframe of your choice.....

plan your trade.........

5) find the instrument which fits in your timerame and your  trading plan

once this is done..... trade the plan.

6) then comes your money management.... initial stoploss , number of lots... first target.....etc

7) lastly if the trade goes in your favour .... how to trail your stoploss to get maximum profit ?

there is no end to learning.....

plan the trade and trade the plan

--- 帖子 #1380 ---
========================

Summary of the below (the short answer): The ideal trigger candle is one that takes place after a pullback and not too far from the origin of that trend.

========================

I can explain where I will enter using a trigger candle, but none of the other items you asked about because a trigger candle is just a good place to enter a trade. The rest is up to the trader.

Let us use a bullish trend as our example. A trigger candle is simply when a price bar opens below all three, or two or just the fast EMA and closes above all of them in "open" space in the direction of the dominant trend.

Notice that all of the trigger candles I have marked close above all three EMAs, in "open" space. When this happens and you decide it represents a good trade, you would usually enter it at the close. In the chart I previously posted (and again below) there are many trigger candles in the direction of the dominant trend. The first two I have marked on the left side are when the three EMAs have not yet become aligned, so if having all three EMAs aligned is important to you, you would not trade these. I would not trade the first one marked anyway because it is too explosive (long) and often price retraces after such a candle. The ideal trigger candles to go for would be the 5th and 6th ones because (i) the EMAs are fully aligned, (ii) they represent a breakout after a pullback, (iii) they are close to the EMAs so they are at the beginning of a new burst of energy and (iv) they are close to the beginning of this trend. The longer the trend lasts the less interested I would be about entering because no trend lasts forever, they all end somewhere, and I don't want to be there when that happens.

In this example I haven't looked at what the higher TFs are doing, but for a valid trade, at least one or both of the HTFs would be bullish as well.
Attached Image (click to enlarge)

--- 帖子 #1381 ---
I am using a new variation of the EMA crossover approach that seems to be working nicely. It's still early days, so time will tell. I will post some trades as they happen to show what I mean.

This variation uses just 2 EMAs (I'm currently using 10 and 24 EMA) and 4 time-frames - monthly, weekly and daily (for trend) and H4 for entries. I wait until the EMAs are all aligned (EMAs all crossed up or all down) and ideally with clean space between the EMAs on all four TFs with the candles above/below them. The alignment across four TFs gives me a high degree of confidence that the trade will at least break even on H4, and more likely, go on to make decent profit most times.

I enter when price is close to the 10 EMA, or is a trigger candle (see previous post). The stop goes beyond the latest swing point, and I move it to break-even around 60 pips. Then I let the trade run until a candle closes in the wrong direction inside the EMAs. That's one way of managing the trade, but it's up to you - many people prefer different approaches to managing a trade.
